  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f24c6835-13d5-4e8f-bf08-7948160de8ee'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_718616379" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/718616379" />
    <a title="Jan Rice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice"><img alt="Jan Rice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497584438p2/5864678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/718616379?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan Rice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan Rice" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice">Jan Rice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5864678-jan-rice?shelf=creativity">creativity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5864678-jan-rice?shelf=theology">theology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5864678-jan-rice?shelf=favorites-best-books">favorites-best-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer718616379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9605279229265365470">Since I have been recommending this book, it seemed like a good idea to read it.<br/><br/>Some time ago my husband read a part of it to me, and it made a deep impression. I've been going around telling people it represents my theology, even though I hadn't read it. And now that I have read it I still say the same. Only it doesn't look like I thought. In my mind's eye it was written in paragraphs in a book of prose. Actually it's more like poetry, and it's a list of numbered rules and regulations intertwin</span>
  <span id="freeText9605279229265365470" style="display:none">Since I have been recommending this book, it seemed like a good idea to read it.<br /><br />Some time ago my husband read a part of it to me, and it made a deep impression. I've been going around telling people it represents my theology, even though I hadn't read it. And now that I have read it I still say the same. Only it doesn't look like I thought. In my mind's eye it was written in paragraphs in a book of prose. Actually it's more like poetry, and it's a list of numbered rules and regulations intertwined with narrative. Kazantzakis says between two intervals of darkness we have a brief interval we call life. How should we see it? What are we supposed to do with it?<br /><br />I'm just going to include some of my favorite parts. That's somewhat arbitrary, and I better watch out or I'll be copying the whole thing. No need for that -- it's available online <a href="http://www.angel.net/~nic/askitiki.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. <br /><br />From <b>The Preparation</b>:<br /><br /><blockquote>Beyond the mind, on the edge of the heart's holy precipice, I proceed, trembling. One foot grips the secure soil, the other gropes in the darkness above the abyss.<br />Behind all appearances, I divine a struggling essence. I want to merge with it.<br />I feel that behind appearances this struggling essence is also striving to merge with my heart. But the body stands between us and separates us. The mind stands between us and separates us.<br />What is my duty? To shatter the body, to rush and merge with the Invisible. To let the mind fall silent that I may hear the Invisible calling.<br />I walk on the rim of the abyss, and I tremble.</blockquote><br /><br />From <b>The March</b>:<br /><br /><blockquote>I hear the savage cry, and I shudder. The agony that ascends within me composes itself, for the first time, into an integral human voice; it turns full face toward me and calls me clearly, with my own name, with the name of my father and my race.<br />This is the moment of greatest crisis. This is the signal for the March to begin. If you do not hear this Cry tearing at your entrails, do not set out.<br />Continue, with patience and submission, your sacred military service in the first, second, and third rank of preparation.<br />And listen: In sleep, in an act of love or of creation, in a proud and disinterested act of yours, or in a profound despairing silence, you may suddenly hear the Cry and set forth.</blockquote><br /><br /><i>The Saviors of God</i> is not conventional theology:<br /><br /><blockquote>"You are not my slave, nor a plaything in my hands. You are not my friend, you are not my child. You are my comrade-in-arms!</blockquote><br /><br />This is the part I heard a long time ago that I never forgot:<br /><br /><blockquote>You are not one; you are a body of troops, One of your faces lights up for a moment under the sun. Then suddenly it vanishes, and another, a younger one, lights up behind you.<br />The race of men from which you come is the huge body of the past, the present, and the future. It is the face itself; you are a passing expression. You are the shadow; it is the meat.<br />You are not free. Myriad invisible hands hold your hands and direct them, When you rise in anger, a great-grandfather froths at your mouth; when you make love, an ancestral caveman growls with lust; when you sleep, tombs open in your memory till your skull brims with ghosts.<br />Your skull is a pit of blood round which the shades of the dead gather in myriad flocks to drink of you and be revived.<br />"Do not die that we may not die," the dead cry out within you. "We had no time to enjoy the women we desired; be in time, sleep with them! We had no time to turn our thoughts into deeds; turn them into deeds! We had no time to grasp and to crystallize the face of our hope; make it firm!<br />"Finish our work! Finish our work! All day and all night we come and go through your body, and we cry out. No, we have not gone, we have not detached ourselves from you, we have not descended into the earth. Deep in your entrails we continue the struggle. Deliver us!"<br /><br />IT IS NOT enough to hear the tumult of ancestors within you. It is not enough to feel them battling at the threshold of your mind. All rush to clutch your warm brain and to climb once more into the light of day.<br />But you must choose with care whom to hurl down again into the chasms of your blood, and whom you shall permit to mount once more into the light and the earth.<br />Do not pity them. Keep vigil over the bottomless gulf of your heart, and choose. You shall say: "This shade is humble, dark, like a beast: send him away! This one is silent and flaming, more living than I: let him drink all my blood.</blockquote><br /><br />More unconventional theology:<br /><br /><blockquote>Our God is not almighty, he is not all-holy, he is not certain that he will conquer, he is not certain that he will be conquered.</blockquote><br /><br />And, for my last quote:<br /><br /><blockquote>LIE IN AMBUSH behind appearances, patiently, and strive to subject them to laws. Thus may you open up roads through chaos and help the spirit on its course.<br />Impose order, the order of your brain, on the flowing anarchy of the world. Incise your plan of battle clearly on the face of the abyss.<br />Contend with the powers of nature, force them to the yoke of superior purpose. Free that spirit which struggles within them and longs to mingle with that spirit which struggles within you.<br />When a man fighting with chaos subdues a series of appearances to the laws of his mind and strictly confines these laws within the boundaries of reason, then the world breathes, the voices are ranged in order, the future becomes clarified, and all the dark and endless quantities of numbers are freed by submitting to mystical quality.<br />With the help of our minds we compel matter to come with us. We divert the direction of descending powers, we alter the course of the current, we transform slavery into freedom.<br />We do not only free God by battling and subduing the visible world about us; we also create God.<br />"Open your eyes," God shouts; "I want to see! Prick up your ears, I want to hear! March in the front ranks: you are my head!"</blockquote><br /><br />In the print edition, there's a 40-page introduction by the translator about the philosophical influences. Kazantzakis' actual text, 90 pages, follows. He started writing it in 1922, published in in 1927. He made some revisions up until '47. My edition is 1960. Parts of it will seem outdated. He uses the masculine pronoun for God and says "mankind," not "humankind." His view of women is not that of a feminist. He's always talking about "fathers" and about "begetting sons." He uses terms like "crusade" innocently. He uses some Christian metaphors, for that is his background (but is not given to anti-Judaism). There are a few touches of socialism or communism. In short, he's a person of his times. <br /><br />I don't think I agree with his vision of two battling forces, one ascending and one descending. Instead, one ascends, struggling and against tough resistance, with the difficulty being the absence of direction or of energy (instead of an opposing force).<br /><br />One might say he's chiseled his little-T torah out of the living rock of his tradition, and because of his skill he has tapped into universality, so the minor dated aspects don't keep his poetry and message from soaring.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9605279229265365470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3200114086" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7906500599005246019">This book is phenomenal and it's hard to write a review that can give it enough credit. I have been looking for a book of this sort since I have read The Death of Ivan Ilyich. I know both are VERY different from one another in so many levels, but at their core they share the same existential angst.<br/><br/>Nikos' existential questions about the matters of life, death and the purpose of humans' existence through out the book, spoke to me on a personal level. The written language is dazzling, and the pictu</span>
  <span id="freeText7906500599005246019" style="display:none">This book is phenomenal and it's hard to write a review that can give it enough credit. I have been looking for a book of this sort since I have read The Death of Ivan Ilyich. I know both are VERY different from one another in so many levels, but at their core they share the same existential angst.<br /><br />Nikos' existential questions about the matters of life, death and the purpose of humans' existence through out the book, spoke to me on a personal level. The written language is dazzling, and the picture that the author tries to paint with each word and sentence is something to reflect on. It's like Nikos has written his own holy book, which in my opinion is THE holy book that deserves to be read, reread and lived by, whether you are a believer or not.<br /><br />The translator did a great job delivering this book in a way I believe was better than the author could ever imagine or wish for.<br /><br />Reading this book was a pleasant surprise and I'm glad I gave it the chance given my previous, not so great experience with the same author.<br /><br />Some of the hidden (or not so hidden) gems in this book (it took me sheer willpower to not quote the entire book lol):<br /><br /><b>1- "We come from a dark abyss, we end in a dark abyss, and we call the luminous interval life."<br /><br />2- "I am the worker of the abyss. I am the spectator of the abyss. I am both theory and practice. I am the law. Nothing beyond me exists."<br /><br />3- "This is all a cruel and futile game, without beginning, without end, without meaning." <br /><br />4- "I have one longing only: to grasp what is hidden behind appearances, to ferret out that<br />mystery which brings me to birth and then kills me, to discover if behind the visible and<br />unceasing stream of the world an invisible and immutable presence is hiding."<br /><br />5- "Who plants us on this earth without asking our<br />permission? Who uproots us from this earth without asking our permission?"<br /><br />6- "I want to find a single justification that I may live and bear this dreadful daily spectacle of<br />disease, of ugliness, of injustice, of death."<br /><br />7- "I once set out from a dark point, the Womb, and now I proceed to another dark point,<br />the Tomb. A power hurls me out of the dark pit and another power drags me irrevocably<br />toward the dark pit."<br /><br />8- "New generations tread on the corpses of their fathers, continue the work<br />above the abyss and struggle to tame the dread mystery. How? By cultivating a single field,<br />by kissing a woman, by studying a stone, an animal, an idea."<br /><br />9- "Where do we come from? Where are we going? What is the meaning of this life? That is<br />what every heart is shouting, what every head is asking as it beats on chaos."<b></b></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="7906500599005246019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_887763645" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887763645" />
    <a title="Eleni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18423957-eleni"><img alt="Eleni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363514617p2/18423957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887763645?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eleni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eleni" href="/user/show/18423957-eleni">Eleni</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887763645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14728122361315516014">Nikos Kazantzakis is easily in my top 3 authors of all time and <b>
  <i>“Asceticism”</i>
</b> (translated as <b>
  <i>“Saviors Of God”</i>
</b> in English – although personally, I find the original Greek title a hundred times better and way more representative of the essence of the book) is <u>clearly his best work,</u> one of the most awe-inspiring and by far THE most genuine and profound philosophical and personal book I have ever read. Ever.<br/><br/><b>
  <i>Asceticism / Saviors Of God</i>
</b> is very short, but a life changing experience. I've ac</span>
  <span id="freeText14728122361315516014" style="display:none">Nikos Kazantzakis is easily in my top 3 authors of all time and <b>
  <i>“Asceticism”</i>
</b> (translated as <b>
  <i>“Saviors Of God”</i>
</b> in English – although personally, I find the original Greek title a hundred times better and way more representative of the essence of the book) is <u>clearly his best work,</u> one of the most awe-inspiring and by far THE most genuine and profound philosophical and personal book I have ever read. Ever.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Asceticism / Saviors Of God</i>
</b> is very short, but a life changing experience. I've actually read it before in college quite quickly, but words as strong as these need to be read again and again for the experience to make sense and I was too young and still too inexperienced a reader to understand that devouring a heavily philosophical book of this kind in a week was completely pointless. <br /><br />I've always disagreed with <b>Oscar Wilde</b> (another top 3 author for me, coincidentally) that <b>
  <i>“if one cannot enjoy reading a book over and over again, there is no use in reading it at all”,</i>
</b> because similarly to my other – definitely top 10 / maybe top 6 – favourite author <b>Nick Hornby,</b> <b>
  <i>“I don’t reread books very often; I’m too conscious of both my ignorance and my mortality”.</i>
</b><br /><br />Nevertheless, this is one case where Oscar is totally spot on. <br />Take your time with <b>
  <i>Asceticism / Saviors Of God</i>
</b> and remember to come back to it at a later time, or don’t bother at all. I remembered and I bothered and I was vindicated.<br /><br />Nikos Kazantzakis believes that we come from an abyss of darkness, we end to an abyss of darkness and the luminous short interval is what we call life. Through <b>
  <i>Asceticism / Saviors Of God,</i>
</b> Kazantzakis digs deep into his soul, then pours out his soul to express his agony and his fear of the unknown and of the inherent oppositions in life. Kazantzakis uses an individual, passionate, poetic yet coherent language to convey the philosophy that governs not only his work, but also his life, as he was a man of vision with a strong stance on hoping, fighting, being free and believing.<br /><br />I cannot vouch for the translated version sadly, but if it’s half as breathtaking as the original text, then it’s a no-brainer, for me it would still be worth every second, every numbness (in awe).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14728122361315516014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13828097902750197027" style="display:none">“WE COME from a dark abyss, we end in a dark abyss, and we call the luminous interval life. As soon as we are born the return begins, at once the setting forth and the coming back; we die in every moment…”<br /><br /><br /><br />This book has to be read again and again…to grasp and to bear constantly in mind the vision N.Kazantzakis wants to give. His beliefs on how to live, how to die, how to hope, how to <br />fight, how to be free, how to find God, how to save God...<br />Using a very strong and individual language, one can feel from the very first page the agony in his writing…and is easily absorbed in it.<br />Sometimes the same phrases are repeated over and over, but I guess this reveals the writer’s solicitude for the world and helps the reader to be more focused.<br />The titles’ chapters may seem simple at first glance… one can find though, that the whole of the writer’s thinking is so complex and powerful…and can’t help but admire the concept and the ultimate structure of this book…<br /><br /><br />“BLESSED BE ALL THOSE WHO HEAR AND RUSH TO FREE YOU, LORD, AND WHO SAY: "ONLY YOU AND I EXIST."<br /><br />BLESSED BE ALL THOSE WHO FREE YOU AND BECOME UNITED WITH YOU, LORD, AND WHO SAY: "YOU AND I ARE ONE."<br /><br />AND THRICE BLESSED BE THOSE WHO BEAR ON THEIR SHOULDERS AND DO NOT BUCKLE UNDER THIS GREAT, SUBLIME, AND TERRIFYING SECRET: THAT EVEN THIS ONE DOES NOT EXIST! “<br /><br />Book for life I would say, but as N.Kazantzakis states ”theory has value only as a preparation, action is the crucial battle.”<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3000084214" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3000084214" />
    <a title="Gastjäle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58816143-gastj-le"><img alt="Gastjäle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471703156p2/58816143.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3000084214?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gastjäle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gastjäle" href="/user/show/58816143-gastj-le">Gastjäle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3000084214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6196360336699231375">This book outlines the spiritual journey of one lost Greek, who is willing even to degrade God in case truth requires it. Kazantzakis searched wide and far, and found suffering, reborn Asians; proactive pie-in-the-sky Christians; Spirits progressing in their Hegelian cycles and warring Nietzschean Übermensches - all of which inspired him yet didn't satisfy his hunger. He find the idea of an all-powerful deity displeasing, perhaps because it indeed is rather OP, and a rather obvious sign of weakn</span>
  <span id="freeText6196360336699231375" style="display:none">This book outlines the spiritual journey of one lost Greek, who is willing even to degrade God in case truth requires it. Kazantzakis searched wide and far, and found suffering, reborn Asians; proactive pie-in-the-sky Christians; Spirits progressing in their Hegelian cycles and warring Nietzschean Übermensches - all of which inspired him yet didn't satisfy his hunger. He find the idea of an all-powerful deity displeasing, perhaps because it indeed is rather OP, and a rather obvious sign of weakness in men to worship such things solely because of such attributes. Nor did he really enjoy the self-mortification bit, not to mention endless rebirth as a new soul, seeking to relieve oneself of suffering. <br /><br />It really seems like Kazantzakis finally submitted to the terrors of life and saw something ultimately real in them. War, pain and death are all written into the bloodstream of the human race, and only by following this stream we can... be saved? Not quite, though Nikos uses the term himself. Humans are destined to help out their maddened God to cast aside the cloak of flesh, to escape the Unnameable abyss, and to rise to a new level – in other words, to perpetuate the recreation of their God. Our "salvation" is to be ground to dust willfully like soldiers, knowing full well we've done our duty out of loyalty and meekness towards this raging force we may as well term God. We must accept our parts, trace our boundaries and crush them with recurring vehemence. Constant self-development until death, all for the sake of the pantheistic force who flees forever inside us all. <br /><br />Sounds a bit glum, innit? Cor! it does indeed, and Kazantzakis makes a point of twisting the knife of mortality in all the wounds he manages to inflict on the reader. We are nothing. Our "life" is but a flash of lightning, soon forgotten. Life is extreme emotions, both joy and fear, but they too are to be destroyed – by us. For God is beyond such frailty, and how can we hope to gain any understanding if we don't follow Him?<br /><br />How can one not love what N.K. is doing here? He creates a new religion entirely for himself! He feels the validity of it so deeply, that he can't help but obey, even though it's a religion of strife and empty flight. He feels all the compelling things in life to emanate from this one source! He is willing to stop at nothing to do this infinitesimal service to his deity of choice. <br /><br />Meanwhile, he keeps battling with solipsism and Hegelian negations, nihilism and false hope. He is intent on finding meaning in the coil of mortals, yet he's ready to accept its possible abhorrence. Or to turn this abhorrence into reverence. All the religions of the world belong to him, all the creatures too. The stars, the galaxies, the whole cosmos seeks to expedite the Great Escape of God! What enthralling thrall to serve such insanity! <br /><br />It's the giddy megalomania and absolute honesty which bursts out of the pages like explosive pus from its pores, intoxicating the reader with its delirious, raw beauty. <i>The Saviors of God</i> is a paradoxical parable of submissive defiance, one that oozes of the primeval breath of ages, passed on through the aeons. It has no Alpha, it has no Omega – it is Omicron, with a spiritus lenis dripping weakly along its curved, neverending flank into the eternity's abyss!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6196360336699231375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2795452884" />
    <a title="Thomas Tyrer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10134668-thomas-tyrer"><img alt="Thomas Tyrer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596498135p2/10134668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2795452884?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Tyrer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Tyrer" href="/user/show/10134668-thomas-tyrer">Thomas Tyrer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19720814-saviors-of-god">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2795452884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7599246559192257116"><strong>Redundant Nihilism</strong><br/><br/>I’ve always been intrigued by “The Last Temptation of Christ” and “Zorba the Greek” and their philosophies of rectifying the spiritual and the physical. So I wanted to enjoy “Saviors of God” and was disappointed when I didn’t. The opening essay establishes the historical and cultural contexts within which the author created the work and yet plodded forward in tedium. I’m well-versed in “deep” and often complex texts, but I found Kazantzakis to make one point - that man and God </span>
  <span id="freeText7599246559192257116" style="display:none"><strong>Redundant Nihilism</strong><br /><br />I’ve always been intrigued by “The Last Temptation of Christ” and “Zorba the Greek” and their philosophies of rectifying the spiritual and the physical. So I wanted to enjoy “Saviors of God” and was disappointed when I didn’t. The opening essay establishes the historical and cultural contexts within which the author created the work and yet plodded forward in tedium. I’m well-versed in “deep” and often complex texts, but I found Kazantzakis to make one point - that man and God constantly rise from the base and fall short of the sublime to seek equilibrium — and then repeat it in a myriad of somewhat similar ways. This was a book that I actually wanted to put down many, many times, and yet because it was so short, remained with it. But in the end, I found it not so much a waste of time as a journey that fell far short of what Kazantzakis had previously inspired. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7599246559192257116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11807326888488912057" style="display:none">For a journey that is driven to its beginnings by words such as "WE COME from a dark abyss, we end in a dark abyss, and we call the luminous interval life.." One expects to find something way beyond a try to present not only a foggy view of the relation ship between man and god, but a rather limiting one, one that's hard to believe a man from whom i expected so much, could actually have.<br /><br />Yet, and to be fair, the mysteriousness this book had to figure out the final condemnation was a thrill, and the fact that such a condemnation doesn't even exist, but is left open for us to make, was rather an impressive reflection of the way in which this man thought, on the way in which this man wrote.. That's why it deserves four stars and a tiny little bit further.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3062857722?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick M." href="/user/show/446570-patrick-m">Patrick M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446570-patrick-m?shelf=spirituality-religion">spirituality-religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3062857722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2606216288382541686">Full disclosure: my only other exposure to Kazantzakis is "The Last Temptation of Christ", which is not a bad book (the Scorsese adaptation is amazing). And translations are tricky things. <br/><br/>But it just fell flat. More than that, once or twice I literally turned away from the book. It's shot through with a panicked fear of chaos and neurotic rage for order, with the kind of joy in destruction that I'm sure appeals to Silicon Valley's masters of the universe. I'm sure someone more versed than me in</span>
  <span id="freeText2606216288382541686" style="display:none">Full disclosure: my only other exposure to Kazantzakis is "The Last Temptation of Christ", which is not a bad book (the Scorsese adaptation is amazing). And translations are tricky things. <br /><br />But it just fell flat. More than that, once or twice I literally turned away from the book. It's shot through with a panicked fear of chaos and neurotic rage for order, with the kind of joy in destruction that I'm sure appeals to Silicon Valley's masters of the universe. I'm sure someone more versed than me in gender theory could expand on the almost coked-up cartoon masculinity on nearly every page. <br /><br />My immediate thought after finishing was "this reminds me of Leviathan from Clive Barker's Hellraiser movies - the Cenobites must have this on their Kindles". Robinson Jeffers did this sort of thing much, much more successfully, with less machismo and more of a sense of timelessness - even that old New England racist Lovecraft more effectively evoked the sense of a universe fundamentally alien to humanity. But maybe the best response is the work of Catherine Keller in recasting the chaos at the very beginning of Genesis as not something to destroy but fundamentally creative and necessary. <br /><br />It's hard to have time for this sort of thing anymore as the world is now full of failed attempts at rationalizing humanity and "externalities" that are on track for leaving modern civilization and the natural world upon which it depends in the dust with Ur and Babylon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2606216288382541686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2979550553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5376866181195500226">I enjoyed reading this and I am glad I did. Kaszantzakis is clearly a "big thinker" and he grapples with some very deep spiritual issues here - almost like spiritual warfare. I don't think one can separate the man from the work in this case. He reminds me a bit of Luis Bunuel in that regard. His art, his life, his beliefs, and his frustrations, they are all wrapped together. You either care about what he is saying or you don't. I doubt many will be on the fence about that. <br/><br/>Sometimes the writing </span>
  <span id="freeText5376866181195500226" style="display:none">I enjoyed reading this and I am glad I did. Kaszantzakis is clearly a "big thinker" and he grapples with some very deep spiritual issues here - almost like spiritual warfare. I don't think one can separate the man from the work in this case. He reminds me a bit of Luis Bunuel in that regard. His art, his life, his beliefs, and his frustrations, they are all wrapped together. You either care about what he is saying or you don't. I doubt many will be on the fence about that. <br /><br />Sometimes the writing doesn't seem all that highly crafted to me - not quite fine literature - but the translation may be to blame for that. But the ideas are both interesting and challenging. If one does not believe in God I would expect this would bore you to tears.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14079702509275730404">Having known Kazantzakis only from his (great) fiction, I found this philosophical exploration of his own life and man's relationship to the universe wonderfully challenging and thought-provoking. Like much of the great philosophy I have read, this book needs to be read slowly and with constant reflection on one's own life and choices. Kazantakis' presentation in short chapters, with short statements of thoughts and inspirations, is perfect for such a journey.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_964185603" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/964185603" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/216885-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503333482p2/216885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/964185603?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/216885-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/216885-david?shelf=science-philosophy">science-philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/216885-david?shelf=mythologyandreligion">mythologyandreligion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer964185603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4646853178146338288"><b>A TOP SHELF review, originally published in the June 20, 2014 edition of <i>Festiva Magazine</i>, the weekly entertainment insert of <a href='http://www.themonitor.com/entertainment/top-shelf-an-evolving-universe/article_48f2c2c0-f7eb-11e3-90a7-001a4bcf6878.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Monitor</a></b><br/><br/>Nikos Kazantzakis is primarily known to the US public through the film adaptations of his novels <i>Zorba the Greek</i> and <i>The Last Temptation</i>. The Cretan-born philosopher and writer was the author and translator of dozens of other works, however, including a sequel to the Greek epic <i>The Odyssey</i>, which he considered to be his greatest achievement.<br/><br/>In 1957, after being</span>
  <span id="freeText4646853178146338288" style="display:none"><b>A TOP SHELF review, originally published in the June 20, 2014 edition of <i>Festiva Magazine</i>, the weekly entertainment insert of <a href="http://www.themonitor.com/entertainment/top-shelf-an-evolving-universe/article_48f2c2c0-f7eb-11e3-90a7-001a4bcf6878.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monitor</a></b><br /><br />Nikos Kazantzakis is primarily known to the US public through the film adaptations of his novels <i>Zorba the Greek</i> and <i>The Last Temptation</i>. The Cretan-born philosopher and writer was the author and translator of dozens of other works, however, including a sequel to the Greek epic <i>The Odyssey</i>, which he considered to be his greatest achievement.<br /><br />In 1957, after being nominated for the Nobel Prize, Kazantzakis lost by a single vote to Albert Camus. A few months later, returning from a tour of Asia, the writer finally succumbed to the leukemia he’d been struggling with for years. His tombstone reads, “I hope for nothing. I fear nothing. I am free.”<br /><br />That fearlessness was the end result of a life-long spiritual quest poignantly detailed in <i>Saviors of God: Spiritual Exercises</i>, translated into English by Kimon Friar. In the pages of this small book, using a heightened tone of sacred worship and ecstasy, Kazantzakis distilled the philosophy that underpins all of his writing: the universe is evolving toward something greater than mere matter, plants, animals, humanity. We have, he insisted, a special duty to this future being now rising through us.<br /><br />He made the highly controversial choice of calling the end product of that evolution “God.” It’s a small wonder the Greek Orthodox Church anathematized him just two years before his death.<br /><br /><i>Saviors of God</i> consists of five parts. In “The Preparation,” Kazantzakis spells out three human duties: to recognize the limitations that separate us from one another, to rebel against those limitations and unite to become the mind of the world, and to struggle for freedom through our collective evolution even while admitting that the universe has no inherent purpose.<br /><br />In “The March,” Kazantzakis presents his vision of the individual contribution to the evolution of God, first by identifying with and then rejecting the ego, allowing the Combatant (our collective drive for freedom and complexity) to flow through and destroy the self; then by gradual identification with and transcendence of one’s culture, of humanity, of the very earth.<br /><br />“The Vision” gives us a glimpse at the gradually evolving hope and freedom that the author calls God, the eventual transmutation of our material selves into “spirit” or “spark,” a virtual being that encompasses the universe with absolute liberty. “The Action” meditates on the need for each person to dedicate him or herself to the advancement of our collective knowledge, freedom and compassion (given that “God” is not all-powerful and must be “saved”).<br /><br />Finally, in “The Silence,” the philosopher reflects on how the spark of each individual mind commingles in the abyss that predates existence, united within the future deity. But he turns this vision on its head with the final, paradoxical axiom: “even this one does not exist!”<br /><br />While likely frustrating to adherents to the big three monotheist religions, this work spoke to me as few other philosophical essays have done. Inspiring and humbling, <i>Saviors of God</i> is made for would-be rational mystics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4646853178146338288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1906621939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1906621939" />
    <a title="Tabarek Raad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27897320-tabarek-raad"><img alt="Tabarek Raad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580383756p2/27897320.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_1730393557" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1730393557" />
    <a title="Janaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1127634-janaka"><img alt="Janaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442585532p2/1127634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1730393557?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janaka" href="/user/show/1127634-janaka">Janaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671202323" href="/book/show/74006.The_Saviors_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1730393557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6313844917008897290">Here Kazantzakis--the author of more seminal works "The Last Temptation of Christ" and "Zorba the Greek"--outlines his spiritual philosophy in an incantatory handbook as mystical as it is poetic. It reads like a manifesto of sorts (perhaps no surprise, given is experience and fascination with Marxism as a spiritual struggle), as each chapter builds upon the last. My only criticism is that there is a definite masculine bent to the ecstatic unfolding of his vision, which fades in and out of the bo</span>
  <span id="freeText6313844917008897290" style="display:none">Here Kazantzakis--the author of more seminal works "The Last Temptation of Christ" and "Zorba the Greek"--outlines his spiritual philosophy in an incantatory handbook as mystical as it is poetic. It reads like a manifesto of sorts (perhaps no surprise, given is experience and fascination with Marxism as a spiritual struggle), as each chapter builds upon the last. My only criticism is that there is a definite masculine bent to the ecstatic unfolding of his vision, which fades in and out of the book--but I think only on one or two occasions does it significantly subtract from the work. I would highly recommend this for fans of Rumi, Rilke, Hölderlin or maybe even Akhmatova.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6313844917008897290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fSPlthCwxIYRyWZt90UQClUqOyReldWVYzPF2ba/XoMZDTfS8pUeRZ+sCD3KwelcMXtIQBzQQnOqfdQRvdJh1g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('87sDkfi4R/FSpCyXBgLGbKQa/Mkpd4Ff+XhkW4Xyjj+XldH1Gp2dMtzBQsc7hj86wEuPrWsyFrkwNnWTjp+xag==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

:\n\nهو عباس الطفل الذي حوّلته ظروف الحياة إلى رجلٍ. تكبّد الفقر، وألم اليُتم، فدخل السوق وعالمه يافعًا يجر عربته، وسريعًا قرر أن يصبح رجلاً (رجلًا صغيرًا)،.. واجه الحياة بشجاعة تفوق عمره وزمنه، تنقّل بين عمل وآخر، وبين دولة وأخرى، آمن بنفسه فنجح. ولكن ما أعطته الحياة بيد أخذته منه بأخرى.\n\nالبداية النصية -الزمكانية - للرواية تبدأ في ثمانينات القرن العشرين في قرية السنابس - البحرين،والنهاية النصية الزمنية تكون مع انتهاء رحلة بطلها وعودته من الخارج إليها بعد ما يقارب الأربعة عقود من الزمن. وبين البداية والنهاية أحداث ووقائع يتداخل فيها العام بالخاص، والداخل بالخارج، فتحضر حرب الخليج وتداعياتها، مثلما تحضر قضية تعنيف المرأة ومآلاتها. ومثلها قضية عمالة الأطفال وإن كانت هذه الرواية طوعية بدافع الإنجاز والترقي إلا أنها تعكس واقعًا مزرياً يسرق مراحل الطفولة وأحلامها بشكل أو بآخر.\n\n *ما يميز هذه اللحم، سوق الطواويش، سوق الذهب، يضاف إليها قائمة من الاطعمة الشهية؛ شاي عبد القادر، عدس مقهى حاجي، سمبوسة بشير، كباب مندلي وتكة أمين.. \n\n *وكل هذا يآتي في استرسال سردي آسر، هو أعمق ما كُتب عن <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11671854239365720774">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________' title='Νίκος Καζαντζάκης' rel='nofollow noopener'>Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br/><br/>Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart o</span>
  <span id="freeText11671854239365720774" style="display:none">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________" title="Νίκος Καζαντζάκης" rel="nofollow noopener">Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br /><br />Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart of fire but with mind ruthless and clear," Kazantzakis wrote in TODA RABA (1934). <br /><br />Several of the author's novels deal with the history and culture of his own country, and the mystical relationship between man and God. In 1957 he lost the Nobel Prize by a single vote to the French writer Albert Camus.</span>
